The Sense Skeptics' Objection: Examples of Snow, Glass, and Cracks
Al-Jurjani presents the skeptics' doubt with three examples of perceived whiteness without actual whiteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how sense skeptics use examples like snow, powdered glass, and a crack in glass to argue that sense perception is fallible.

Core Idea
Sense skeptics argue that what we see as white may be an illusion of light and reflection.

Before Al-Jurjani responds to the sense skeptics, he first presents their strongest doubts. They use three increasingly clear examples to show that our senses can be deceived. This lesson walks through those examples and the logic behind the skeptics' claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage that presents the skeptics' doubt. Notice the three examples and the reasoning behind each.
The passage is dense with technical terms; read the translation alongside.
2
Identify the three examples
Find the examples: snow, powdered glass, and the crack in thick glass. Note how each is described as appearing white despite no actual whiteness.
The examples are presented in order of increasing clarity.
3
Understand why each example is clearer
For each example, understand why the skeptics consider it a stronger proof of sense error. Snow might involve mixture; powdered glass has no mixture; the crack involves no parts at all.
This progression strengthens the skeptics' argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - في إثبات العلوم الضرورية
وقولهم سببه ) أي سبب أنا نراه أبيض ( مداخلة الهواء ( المضيء بالأشعة الفائضة من الأجرام النيرة ( للأجزاء الشفافة) المتصغرة جداً ) وتعاكس الأضواء من سطوحها الصغار بعضها إلى بعض، فإن الضوء المنعكس يرى كلون البياض، ألا ترى أن الشمس إذا أشرقت على الماء وانعكس شعاعها منه إلى الجدار يرى الجدار كأنه أبيض فإذا كثر الانعكاس بين الأجزاء الرشية جداً تخيل ما على سطوحها من الضوء بیاضا في الغاية ( من النمط الأول ( أي من قبيل بيان أسباب الغلط، وقد عرفت أنه لا فائدة فيه على ما قرره ) وأظهر منه ) أي من الثلج في الدلالة على غلط الحس ( الزجاج المدقوق ) دقاً ناعماً فإنه يرى أبيض ولا بياض هناك (و) إنما كان أظهر لأنه (لم يحدث له مزاج يحدث ذلك المزاج (البياض) المشروط به عندهم ( فإن أجزاءه صلبة يابسة ) ومتفقة في الصور والكيفيات ( لا تفاعل بينها ) لعدم الالتصاق، واتفاق الضورة والكيفية فكيف يتصور حدوث المزاج فيه؟ مع كونه مشروطاً عندهم بالتفاعل وأما الثلج ففيه أجزاء مائية ،وهوائية، فجاز أن يتوهم فيما بينهما تفاعل (وأظهر منهما ) في الدلالة على غلط الحس (موضع الشق من الزجاج الثخين الشفاف) فإنه يرى أبيض ولا بياض هناك قطعا ( إذ ليس ثمة إلا الزجاج والهواء لمحتقن في ذلك الشق ( وشيء منهما غير ملون ) أي ليس شيء منهما بملون، وإنما كان أظهر منهما إذ ليس هناك أجزاء متصغرة يتوهم تفاعلها ( والجواب ) عن شبه هذه الفرقة ( أن مقتضاه ) أي مقتضى ما ذكرتم من الشبه الدالة على أن حكم
الحس لا يعتبر في الكليات ولا في الجزئيات أن لا يجزم العقل) بحكم كلي أو جزئي (بمجرده) أي بمجرد الحس والإحساس به أما في الكلي فلعدم تعلق الحس بجميع الأفراد، وأما في الجزئي فلأنه قد يغلط فيه (و) نحن نقول به) فإن جزم العقل ليس يحصل في الكليات ولا في الجزئيات بمجرد الإحساس بالحواس، بل لا ذلك من أمور أخر توجب الجزم كما مر فإذا لم توجد تلك الأمور في بعض الصور لم يكن من العقل جزم، وكان احتمال الخطأ هناك قائماً ( لا أن لا يوثق (بجزمه ) أي بجزم العقل (بما جزم به من الأحكام الكلية أو الجزئية على المحسوسات بحصول تلك الأمور مع الإحساس في هذه الصور، وكيف لا يوثق بجزمه هاهنا ؟ مع أن بديهته شاهدة بصحته وانتفاء الغلط عنه كما في قولنا الشمس مضيئة والنار حارة ( وكونه محتملاً) هو مرفوع عطفاً على أن لا يوثق أي لا الوثوق بجزمه، وكون جزمه محتملاً للغلط، وتوهم كونه مجروراً معطوفاً على بجزمه أي لا أن لا يوثق بكون الجزم محتملاً للحصول في بعض المحسوسات، بأن تنضم فيه إلى الحس أمور توجب الجزم باطل قطعاً، إذ لا فائدة في هذا الموضع لذكر كون الجزم محتملاً للوجود ، ولا لعدم الوثوق بذلك الاحتمال
The Fourth Observatory - On the Proof of Necessary Sciences
And their saying: its cause (i.e., the cause of our seeing it white) is the interpenetration of air (illuminated by rays flowing from luminous bodies) into the transparent particles that are very small, and the reflection of lights from their small surfaces to one another, for the reflected light is seen as the color of whiteness. Do you not see that when the sun shines on water and its ray is reflected from it to the wall, the wall is seen as if it were white? And when reflection increases among the very moist particles, what is on their surfaces of light is imagined as extreme whiteness. (This is of the first type, i.e., of the category of explaining the causes of error, and you have known that there is no benefit in it according to what he established.) And clearer than it (i.e., than snow in indicating the error of sense) is powdered glass (finely ground), for it is seen as white while there is no whiteness there. It is clearer because no mixture has occurred in it that would produce that mixture (the whiteness) conditioned by them. For its parts are hard and dry, uniform in form and qualities, with no interaction between them due to lack of adhesion and uniformity of form and quality. So how can the occurrence of mixture be imagined in it? Especially since it is conditioned by them with interaction. As for snow, it contains watery and airy parts, so it is possible to imagine interaction between them. And clearer than these two is the location of the crack in thick transparent glass, for it is seen as white with absolutely no whiteness there. For there is nothing there but glass and air confined in that crack, and nothing of them is colored. Rather, it is clearer than them because there are no small particles whose interaction could be imagined. (The answer) to the doubts of this group (is that its implication, i.e., the implication of what you have mentioned of the doubts indicating that the judgment of sense is not reliable in universals or particulars, is that the intellect does not make a judgment, universal or particular, by sense alone. We affirm this. For the intellect's certainty does not occur in universals or particulars merely by sense perception, but rather by other matters that necessitate certainty, as has passed. So if those matters are not present in some cases, there is no certainty from the intellect, and the possibility of error remains. (It does not imply) that one should not trust the intellect's certainty in what it has judged of universal or particular judgments about perceptibles when those matters are present along with sense perception in these cases. How can one not trust its certainty here, when its intuition (badīha) testifies to its correctness and the absence of error, as in our saying 'the sun is luminous' and 'fire is hot'? And (the statement) that its certainty is possible (i.e., possible of error) is incorrect, because it is raised in conjunction with 'that one should not trust' — i.e., there is no trust in its certainty, and that its certainty is possible of error. And the misunderstanding that it is genitive and conjoined to 'by its certainty' — i.e., that there is no trust in the possibility of certainty occurring in some perceptibles, by sense being joined by matters that necessitate certainty — is absolutely false, for there is no benefit in this context in mentioning the possibility of certainty existing, nor in the lack of trust in that possibility.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (shubha wa jawāb) from the fourth observatory on necessary sciences. The author first presents the skeptics' doubt about sense perception. They argue that whiteness is not an inherent property but an illusion caused by light reflection and interpenetration among fine particles. To prove sense error, they cite three examples: snow, powdered glass, and a crack in thick glass. Snow appears white but might be due to mixture of water and air; powdered glass appears white without any actual mixture because its dry, uniform parts cannot interact; the crack in glass appears white even though there are only glass and air, both colorless. Each example is presented as progressively clearer proof of sense fallibility. The author then gives the response: the skeptics' examples only show that the intellect does not judge by sense alone in all cases. In universals, sense cannot cover all individuals; in particulars, sense may err. However, when additional factors (such as repeated observation, absence of counter-evidence, or intuitive certainty) are present, the intellect's judgment is trustworthy. The author emphasizes that the correct conclusion is not to distrust all intellectual judgments about perceptibles, but to recognize that certainty arises only when these extra conditions are met. He illustrates with the intuitive certainty that the sun is luminous and fire is hot. Any claim that such certainty is merely probable is rejected as having no benefit in this context.

Key Terms
الحس (al-ḥiss)
الغلط (al-ghalat)
المزاج (al-mizāj)
التفاعل (al-tafāʿul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the senses are always reliable and give certain knowledge about the world.
Correction: The skeptics use these examples to show that sense perception can be deceived; but this only shows that sense alone is not sufficient for certainty, not that all sense-based judgments are false.

Checkpoint
Why is the crack in glass considered a clearer example of sense error than snow?
A) Because the crack is smaller than snow particles.
B) Because there are no particles whose interaction could be imagined.
C) Because glass is more transparent than snow.
D) Because the crack is only visible in thick glass.

Key Takeaway
The skeptics use three examples to argue that sense perception can be deceived, each example more convincing than the last.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at a white wall or a piece of paper, have you ever considered that what you see as 'white' might be merely an illusion of light? How does the possibility that your most basic visual perceptions could be wrong affect your confidence in what you think you know about the world around you?
?
The skeptics use ordinary examples—snow, powdered glass, a crack—to argue that your senses lie to you. Which area of your life do you most rely on your senses to give you certainty? Are you willing to admit that this 'certainty' might be built on nothing more than reflected light and imagined interaction?
?
If you have ever dismissed a theological claim because it seemed to contradict 'common sense' or 'what you see with your own eyes,' what does this passage teach you about the arrogance of trusting your senses over revelation? Will you now humble yourself to accept truths that your eyes cannot verify?

The Response: Intellect Does Not Judge by Sense Alone
Al-Jurjani responds to the sense skeptics, clarifying that intellect judges with certainty only when additional conditions are met.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani’s response to the sense skeptics: that the intellect's certainty comes from additional conditions, not sense alone.

Core Idea
The intellect judges with certainty only when sense is supported by other factors.

Al-Jurjani now answers the skeptics. He argues that their examples only show that sense alone is insufficient for certainty, not that intellectual judgments about perceptibles are never trustworthy. He explains what additional conditions give the intellect certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic passage of the response carefully. Note the structure: it first states the implication of the skeptics' examples, then affirms part of it while rejecting the overgeneralization.
The response is a shubha-wajab (objection-answer) structure.
2
Understand the skeptics' implication
The skeptics claim that sense is unreliable in both universal and particular judgments. Al-Jurjani shows that this implies the intellect does not judge by sense alone—which is true.
3
Grasp the distinction
Al-Jurjani distinguishes between two possible conclusions: (1) intellect does not judge by sense alone (true) and (2) intellect's judgments about perceptibles are never trustworthy (false). The examples only support the first.
4
See the example of intuitive certainty
Al-Jurjani gives examples like 'the sun is luminous' and 'fire is hot' as cases where the intellect has certainty due to intuition (badīha) and absence of error.
These examples show that when additional conditions are met, the intellect's judgment is trustworthy.

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (shubha wa jawāb) from the fourth observatory on necessary sciences. The author first presents the skeptics' doubt about sense perception. They argue that whiteness is not an inherent property but an illusion caused by light reflection and interpenetration among fine particles. To prove sense error, they cite three examples: snow, powdered glass, and a crack in thick glass. Snow appears white but might be due to mixture of water and air; powdered glass appears white without any actual mixture because its dry, uniform parts cannot interact; the crack in glass appears white even though there are only glass and air, both colorless. Each example is presented as progressively clearer proof of sense fallibility. The author then gives the response: the skeptics' examples only show that the intellect does not judge by sense alone in all cases. In universals, sense cannot cover all individuals; in particulars, sense may err. However, when additional factors (such as repeated observation, absence of counter-evidence, or intuitive certainty) are present, the intellect's judgment is trustworthy. The author emphasizes that the correct conclusion is not to distrust all intellectual judgments about perceptibles, but to recognize that certainty arises only when these extra conditions are met. He illustrates with the intuitive certainty that the sun is luminous and fire is hot. Any claim that such certainty is merely probable is rejected as having no benefit in this context.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the intellect can never be certain about perceptibles because sense may err.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that when additional conditions (like intuition) are present, the intellect achieves certainty.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Jurjani's response, what is required for the intellect to achieve certainty about perceptibles?
A) Only sense perception
B) Sense perception plus additional conditions that necessitate certainty
C) The absence of any possibility of error
D) Logical deduction alone

Key Takeaway
The response shows that the intellect can achieve certainty about perceptibles when sense is accompanied by conditions like intuition or repeated verification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you were absolutely certain about something you sensed—like the heat of fire? Now think: have you ever been certain about a belief without checking it against your intuition or deeper reasoning? What would it take for you to question that certainty?
?
The lesson says that when certain conditions are met, the intellect's judgment is error-free. In your own life, where are you most prone to trusting your senses alone—perhaps in judging people or situations—and ignoring the need for additional verification? Will you change that starting today?
?
Al-Jurjani gives 'the sun is luminous' as an example of intuitive certainty. But consider: have you ever taken for granted a truth that you never really examined? If your certainty about that truth were challenged, would you be able to defend it with the kind of additional matters this lesson demands?

The Third Group: Doubters of Self-Evident Truths
Al-Jurjani introduces the third group of skeptics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position of the third group of skeptics who question only self-evident truths while accepting sense data.

Core Idea
Some skeptics accept sense data but doubt self-evident truths.

After examining groups who doubt sense perception, al-Jurjani presents a third group with a different approach. They acknowledge the reliability of the senses but claim that self-evident truths are weaker. This passage sets up their claim for later refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the group is defined by what they object to and what they accept.
The phrase 'only' (فقط) is key to their selectivity.
2
Identify the group's stance
What do they accept? What do they doubt? They accept sense perceptions but doubt self-evident truths.
3
Understand the claim of weakness
They claim self-evident truths are 'weaker' (أضعف) than sense perceptions. Think about what epistemic weakness might mean.
This is their core assertion.
4
Consider the context
This is the third group of skeptics. Recall or anticipate that other groups doubted sense perceptions. This group is distinct.
Connects to broader discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الثالثة القادحون في البديهيات فقط ) أي لا في الحسيات فإنهم معترفون بها (قالوا: هي أضعف من الحسيات
The third group: those who object only to self-evident truths (i.e., not to sense perceptions, for they acknowledge them). They said: They are weaker than sense perceptions.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage introduces the third group of skeptics (al-firqa al-thālitha). Unlike the earlier groups who doubted sense perceptions, this group accepts sense data as certain. They only question the self-evident truths (al-badīhiyyāt), such as logical axioms. Their claim is that self-evident truths are 'weaker' (aḍʿaf) than sense perceptions in epistemological strength. The phrase 'weaker' indicates that they consider sense perceptions more reliable, perhaps because self-evident truths are derived from sense or are more abstract. The author does not yet provide the counter-argument in this passage; it only states the group's position. The text is a fragment from the larger discussion.

Key Terms
البديهيات (al-badīhiyyāt)
الحسيات (al-ḥissiyyāt)
أضعف (aḍʿaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All skeptics deny sense perception entirely.
Correction: This third group explicitly acknowledges sense perceptions but doubts only self-evident truths.

Checkpoint
What do the third group of skeptics claim about self-evident truths?
A) They are stronger than sense perceptions.
B) They are equal to sense perceptions.
C) They are weaker than sense perceptions.
D) They are non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The third group of skeptics holds that self-evident truths are epistemically weaker than sense perceptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are certain of a truth through reason or revelation, but your senses or desires suggest otherwise, which do you tend to follow? What has that cost you?
?
The skeptics claimed self-evident truths are 'weaker' than sense perceptions. Is there a truth you know to be true but struggle to act on because worldly experiences seem more pressing? Will you commit today to prioritizing that truth?

The Role of Sense Perception in Certainty
Discusses sense perception and rational certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sense perception alone is insufficient for certainty and how the intellect's certainty depends on additional factors beyond mere sensation.

Core Idea
Sense perception alone does not yield certainty; reason's certainty comes from other factors.

This passage from the 'Fourth Observatory' of a classical kalam work addresses a skeptical claim that sense perception does not count for certainty. The author affirms the claim with nuance, explaining that rational certainty requires additional factors beyond the senses. He then clarifies that when those factors are present, the intellect's judgment is trustworthy. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
The passage begins by stating that sense is not considered in universals nor particulars. Notice the reasoning for each.
2
Understand the author's affirmation and nuance
The author says 'We say it' – he agrees but explains that the intellect's certainty comes from other factors. See how he distinguishes between cases where those factors are present or absent.
3
Analyze the refutation of a misinterpretation
The author rejects an incorrect reading that would undermine trust in the intellect's judgment. Pay attention to the examples of intuition (badīhah) like 'the sun is luminous'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الحس لا يعتبر في الكليات ولا في الجزئيات أن لا يجزم العقل( بحكم كلي أو جزئي (بمجرده) أي بمجرد الحس والإحساس به أما في الكلي فلعدم تعلق الحس بجميع الأفراد، وأما في الجزئي فلأنه قد يغلط فيه (و) نحن نقول به) فإن جزم العقل ليس يحصل في الكليات ولا في الجزئيات بمجرد الإحساس بالحواس، بل لا ذلك من أمور أخر توجب الجزم كما مر فإذا لم توجد تلك الأمور في بعض الصور لم يكن من العقل جزم، وكان احتمال الخطأ هناك قائماً ( لا أن لا يوثق (بجزمه ) أي بجزم العقل (بما جزم به من الأحكام الكلية أو الجزئية على المحسوسات بحصول تلك الأمور مع الإحساس في هذه الصور، وكيف لا يوثق بجزمه هاهنا ؟ مع أن بديهته شاهدة بصحته وانتفاء الغلط عنه كما في قولنا الشمس مضيئة والنار حارة ( وكونه محتملاً) هو مرفوع عطفاً على أن لا يوثق أي لا الوثوق بجزمه، وكون جزمه محتملاً للغلط، وتوهم كونه مجروراً معطوفاً على بجزمه أي لا أن لا يوثق بكون الجزم محتملاً للحصول في بعض المحسوسات، بأن تنضم فيه إلى الحس أمور توجب الجزم باطل قطعاً، إذ لا فائدة في هذا الموضع لذكر كون الجزم محتملاً للوجود ، ولا لعدم الوثوق بذلك الاحتمال
The Fourth Observatory: On Establishing Necessary Sciences. The sense (al-ḥiss) is not considered in universals (kulliyyāt) nor in particulars (juzʾiyyāt) that the intellect (al-ʿaql) does not make a judgment (jazm) regarding a universal or particular ruling (ḥukm) by it alone, i.e., by mere sense and perception. As for universals, because the sense does not encompass all individuals; and as for particulars, because it may err in them. And we say it: for the judgment of the intellect does not occur in universals nor in particulars by mere sense perception, but rather by other matters that necessitate judgment as previously stated. So if those matters are not present in some cases, then there is no judgment from the intellect, and the possibility of error remains there. It is not that one should not trust in its judgment regarding what it has judged of universal or particular rulings about sensed things due to the presence of those matters along with sense in these cases. How could one not trust its judgment here, while its intuition (badīhah) testifies to its correctness and the absence of error, as in our statement: the sun is luminous and fire is hot. And (its being probable) is raised as a conjunction to 'that one should not trust' i.e., the lack of trust in its judgment and the possibility of its judgment being prone to error. And the illusion that it is genitive, connected to 'in its judgment', i.e., that one should not trust that the judgment is likely to occur in some sensed things by way of matters that necessitate judgment being joined to sense, is absolutely false; for there is no benefit in this place in mentioning that the judgment is likely to exist, nor in the lack of trust in that probability.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-response. It opens by defining a position by some skeptics: that the sense does not count in universals or particulars, meaning the intellect cannot make a certain judgment based solely on sense perception. The reason given is that for universals, sense cannot grasp all individuals; for particulars, sense may err. Then the author responds: 'We say it'—i.e., we affirm this position, but only as a description of what actually happens, not as a denial of rational certainty. He explains that the intellect's certainty in universals and particulars does not come from sense alone but from other factors (umūru ukhar) that necessitate judgment. If those factors are absent, then indeed the intellect does not judge and error is possible. Then he clarifies that this does not mean we should not trust the intellect when it does judge, because when those factors are present along with sense, the intellect's intuition (badīhah) testifies to correctness, as in obvious examples. Finally, he rejects an illusion that the text could be interpreted to mean that the judgment itself is merely probable in some cases, stating that such an interpretation is absolutely false and pointless. The passage follows the order of: statement of claim, affirmation with nuance, explanation of the real cause of certainty, and refutation of a misinterpretation.

جزم العقل
البديهة
المحسوسات
الأمور الأخر
الكليات
الجزئيات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the author denies rational certainty altogether.
Correction: The author affirms rational certainty when additional factors (beyond sense) are present.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the intellect sometimes not make a certain judgment in particular sensory perceptions?
A) Because sense perception is always fallible
B) Because it may not be accompanied by the other factors that necessitate judgment
C) Because the intellect is incapable of judging particulars
D) Because the senses do not provide any information about particulars

Key Takeaway
The intellect's certainty in sense-based judgments depends on additional rational factors beyond mere sensation; when those factors are present, the judgment is trustworthy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold with absolute certainty about the physical world (e.g., that fire burns). Is your certainty derived from your senses alone, or is there a rational judgment beyond mere sensation? How does this lesson reveal the hidden assumptions behind your everyday certainties?
?
The passage says that when 'other matters' are present, intuition testifies to correctness. But when you feel intuitively certain about something, are you sure that intuition is not just a feeling of familiarity? How can you distinguish between genuine rational intuition and mere subjective impression? What practical step can you take to verify your intuitions as you go through today?
?
The author refutes the idea that rational judgments are merely probable. Yet in your own life, do you often settle for 'probably true' when it comes to religious matters? What would it take for you to seek the kind of certainty described here—a certainty beyond mere sense and probability? Are you willing to examine the 'other matters' that should underlie your convictions?

The First Self-Evident Truth and Three Dependent Principles
Commentary on the first self-evident truth and three dependent principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first self-evident truth (the principle of non-contradiction) and the three logical principles that depend on it, including their proofs and the commentary that clarifies their meaning and refutes objections.

Core Idea
The principle of non-contradiction is the most evident truth, and three principles—whole greater than part, equality to same thing—depend on it.

This lesson introduces the foundational principle of all reasoning: that a thing either is or is not. This self-evident truth is the strongest and most evident of all axioms. Upon it depend three other principles that are essential for rational thought: the whole is greater than the part, and things equal to the same thing are equal to each other. The passage also addresses objections and clarifies the precise meaning of these principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the first self-evident truth
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the first self-evident truth ('A thing either is or is not') and notice how it is described as the most evident and strongest of axioms.
This is the principle of non-contradiction.
2
Understand the three dependent principles and their proofs
The passage states three principles that depend on the first truth: (1) the whole is greater than the part, (2) things equal to the same thing are equal. Each is accompanied by a brief proof showing that denying them leads to contradiction.
These proofs rely on the first principle.
3
Examine the commentary on the disjunction
The commentary clarifies the meaning of 'is' and 'is not' as more general than predicative/copulative, and explains that the separation is real. It also refutes a view from Sharh al-Maqasid.
This part deepens understanding of the first principle.
4
Consider the objection and answer regarding equality
An objection is raised about distances of fast and slow motion being equal to time but not to each other. The response denies equality in quantity and explains correspondence instead.
This refines the application of the equality principle.

Regarding certainty, our statement: 'A thing either is or is not' – meaning the disjunction between negation and affirmation, that they do not combine and do not both disappear – and that it is not certain. As for the first, namely that it is the most evident and strongest of self-evident truths, it is because those who acknowledge self-evident truths exemplify them with this disjunction between negation and affirmation. And three others depend on it. The first of the three dependent is our statement: 'The whole is greater than the part.' Otherwise (i.e., if it were not greater), then the other part would be considered in the whole because it is a part of the whole, and not considered in it because satisfaction is achieved by the first part, since it is assumed that the whole is not more than it, so negation and affirmation would combine. The second of those three is our statement: 'Things equal (in quantity, for example) to one thing are equal (in quantity).' Otherwise (i.e., if they were not equal in quantity), then their reality (in quantity) would be one. His saying: 'I mean the disjunction etc.' He indicated by carefulness that what is meant by 'is' and 'is not' is more general than predicative and copulative, as is understood from the explanation of the dependence of the three propositions, and that the separation between them is real. And what is meant by negation and affirmation is nonexistence and existence, not mental abstraction and affirmation, because they are removed from doubt. In that is a refutation of what is in Sharh al-Maqasid when it made the mentioned statement an interpretation of the mentioned disjunction. His saying: 'The whole is greater than the part' means the quantitative whole is greater than its quantitative part. His saying: 'Things equal in quantity, for example, to one thing' – it was said: The distance of fast motion and slow motion are not equal while they are equal in quantity to one thing, namely their time. The answer is to deny their equality to the time of the two motions in quantity, whether the distance is considered a substance or an accident, because there is no room for numerical equality in terms of parts due to their lack, nor for quantitative equality due to the lack of homogeneity between distance and time. If distance is made a substance, it is obvious; if it is made an accident, because it is a fixed magnitude unlike time. Yes, they correspond to time in the sense that when a part of time ceases, a part of the distance ceases. If this is made the meaning of equality, there is no impossibility that the two mentioned distances are also equal in the sense that when a part of one ceases, a part of the other ceases, even if the hypothetical parts in each level differ in magnitude. His saying: 'Their reality in quantity is one because of their equality to that thing' – there is a flaw in this explanation.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the first self-evident truth and three principles that depend on it, as part of a discussion on necessary sciences. The passage begins by stating that the first self-evident truth is the principle of non-contradiction: 'A thing either is or is not.' This is a disjunction between affirmation and negation such that they cannot both be true nor both false. The commentator notes that this principle is the most evident and strongest of self-evident truths because even those who deny self-evident truths use it as an example. Then three other principles are mentioned that depend on this first truth: (1) The whole is greater than the part. The proof is given: if the whole were not greater than the part, then the other part would both be considered (because it is a part) and not considered (because the first part suffices), leading to contradiction. (2) Things equal to the same thing are equal to each other. The proof: if they were not equal, then their reality in quantity would be one (because each equals the same thing), which is contradictory. The commentary then elaborates on the meaning of the disjunction: 'is' and 'is not' are more general than predicative or copulative uses, and the separation is real. Negation and affirmation here mean nonexistence and existence, not mental operations. This refutes a view in Sharh al-Maqasid. Next, the passage clarifies that 'the whole is greater than the part' refers to quantitative whole and part. Then it addresses an objection about motion distances: two distances might be equal to the same time but not equal to each other. The answer is that they are not equal to the time in quantity because distance and time are not homogeneous; they only correspond in the sense of simultaneous cessation, which does not require equality. Finally, a flaw is noted in the explanation that 'their reality in quantity is one because of their equality to that thing'.

الترديد بين النفي والإثبات
الكل أعظم من الجزء
الأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle of non-contradiction is merely a logical rule of thumb, not necessarily the most evident axiom.
Correction: The passage asserts it is the most evident and strongest of self-evident truths, used as the prime example even by those who reject other axioms.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first and strongest self-evident truth?
A) The whole is greater than the part.
B) Things equal to the same thing are equal to each other.
C) A thing either is or is not.
D) The disjunction between negation and affirmation.

Key Takeaway
The principle of non-contradiction is the foundation of all reasoning, and from it derive the principles that the whole is greater than the part and that things equal to the same thing are equal.

?

The First Self-Evident Proposition: The Whole is Greater than the Part
Commentary on the first self-evident proposition: the whole is greater than the part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the first self-evident proposition 'the whole is greater than the part', including its quantitative scope, a notable objection, and the distinction between equality and correspondence.

Core Idea
The whole is greater than the part when both are considered in terms of quantity.

The commentator begins by clarifying the first self-evident proposition, specifying that it concerns quantitative wholes and parts. He then addresses a criticism of an included example and resolves an apparent counterexample involving fast and slow motion. This lesson unpacks these subtleties to deepen your understanding of this foundational principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the commentary's flow.
Notice the commentator's critical tone regarding the word 'for example'.
2
Understand the clarification
The commentator specifies that 'the whole' means the quantitative whole. Why is this specification important? Reflect on whether the proposition holds for non-quantitative wholes.
3
Examine the objection and response
The objection claims that distances of fast and slow motion are unequal despite being equal in time. The response distinguishes between equality and correspondence. Analyze how this resolves the objection.
Correspondence means proportional increase and decrease, not numerical equality.
4
Consider the additional remarks
The commentator dismisses a claim about sensation and intellection, and notes a future discussion on preparedness. These are less central but show the depth of engagement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الكل أعظم من الجزء ) أي الكل المقداري أعظم من جزئه المقداري . قوله : ( مثلاً) لا فائدة فيه لأن المساواة واللامساواة خاصة للكم لا توجد في غيره إلا بالتبع، وما قيل أن مسافتي الحركة السريعة والبطيئة غير متساويتين مع تساويهما لشيء واحد، أعني زمانهما، فمنشؤه عدم الفرق بين المساواة والانطباق فلان كلا من المسافة والحركة، والزمان منطبق على الآخر بمعنى أنه يزيد بزيادة الآخر وينتقص بانتقاصه. غير ولا شك أن الإحساس أقوى من التعقل بهذا المعنى تعسف لا يفهم من العبارة، هذا وفي جعل الاستعداد شرطاً لحصول الكمال كلام ستطلع عليه في بحث العلة والمعلول .
His saying: 'The whole is greater than the part' – meaning the quantitative whole is greater than its quantitative part. His saying: 'for example' – there is no benefit in it, because equality and inequality are specific to quantity and are not found in other than it except by following. And what is said that the distances of fast and slow motion are not equal while they are equal to one thing, namely their time, its origin is the failure to distinguish between equality and correspondence, because each of distance, motion, and time corresponds to the other in the sense that it increases with the increase of the other and decreases with its decrease. But there is no doubt that sensation is stronger than intellection in this sense – a strained interpretation not understood from the expression. This, and concerning making preparedness a condition for attaining perfection, there is a discussion that you will encounter in the investigation of cause and effect.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage provides commentary on the first self-evident proposition 'the whole is greater than the part'. It begins by specifying that 'whole' here means quantitative whole, and 'part' means its quantitative part. The commentator then criticizes the inclusion of the word 'for example' in the proposition, arguing that equality and inequality are properties specific to quantity and are not found in other categories except derivatively, so the example is redundant. Next, an objection is addressed: it is claimed that the distances covered by fast and slow motion are not equal to each other even though they are equal to their common time. The response is that this confusion arises from failing to distinguish between equality and correspondence (inṭibāq). The distance, motion, and time correspond in the sense that they increase and decrease together, but this does not imply quantitative equality. The commentator then rejects as far-fetched the claim that sensation is stronger than intellection in this context, stating that such an interpretation is not understood from the expression. Finally, the passage notes that the issue of making preparedness a condition for attaining perfection will be discussed later in the investigation of cause and effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proposition 'the whole is greater than the part' applies to any whole, even conceptual ones, and equality and correspondence are the same.
Correction: The proposition specifically concerns quantitative wholes and parts; equality is exact sameness while correspondence is proportional co-variation.

Key Takeaway
The first self-evident proposition 'the whole is greater than the part' is properly understood in terms of quantity, and the distinction between equality and correspondence resolves apparent counterexamples.

The Second Self-Evident Proposition: Equality, Homogeneity, and Application
Commentary on the second self-evident proposition with objection and applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second self-evident proposition (things equal to the same thing are equal), the classic objection regarding fast and slow motion distances, its resolution by distinguishing equality from correspondence, and the broad application of this principle in geometry, arithmetic, time, and natural philosophy.

Core Idea
Equal to the same means equal to each other only when quantities are homogeneous.

This passage from the commentary on the second self-evident proposition addresses a classic objection: the distances of fast and slow motion are equal to the same time, yet they are unequal to each other. The commentator resolves this by distinguishing between equality (requiring homogeneity) and correspondence. It then explores how this principle and the whole-greater-than-part principle underlie disciplines like geometry, arithmetic, time, and natural bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to grasp the objection, response, and applications.
Pay attention to the objection about fast/slow motion distances and the commentator's use of 'correspondence' (muṭābaqa).
2
Analyze the objection and its resolution
Identify the objection: distances of fast and slow motion are equal to the same time but unequal to each other. Notice how the commentator denies equality due to lack of homogeneity between distance and time.
The commentator mentions that numerical equality is impossible because there are no 'parts' of time, and quantitative equality is impossible because distance and time are not homogeneous.
3
Understand the definition of equality
The commentator states that equality in quantity means unity in quantity. This makes the proposition almost a tautology: if things are equal (united) to the same, they are united to each other.
This definitional approach is key to understanding the self-evidence of the proposition.
4
Explore the applications
The passage lists applications: geometry, arithmetic, time (as a measure), and natural bodies (rarefaction, condensation, etc.). Note how these depend on the two premises: whole greater than part and equality to the same.
The commentator shows that many scientific investigations rely on these self-evident propositions.
5
Reflect on the warning
The passage ends with a caution against begging the question: unity of quantity is defined as equality, so the argument risks circularity. Reflect on this subtlety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الأشياء المساوية في الكمية مثلاً لشيء واحد قيل مسافة الحركة السريعة والبطيئة غير متساوية مع أنهما متساويتان في الكمية لشيء واحد أعني زمانهما والجواب منع مساواتهما لزمان الحركتين في الكمية سواء اعتبر المسافة جوهراً أو عرضاً، إذ لا مساغ للمساواة العددية بحسب الأجزاء لعدمها ولا للمساواة المقدارية لعدم المجانسة بين المسافة والزمان، أما إذا جعلت المسافة جوهراً فظاهر، وأما إذا جعلت عرضاً فلان مقدار قار بخلاف الزمان، نعم هما مطابقتان للزمان بمعنى أنه إذا انقرض جزء من الزمان انقرض جزء من المسافة، ولو جعل هذا معنى المساواة، لم يمتنع كون المسافتين المذكورتين متساويتين أيضاً بمعنى أنه إذا انقرض جزء من إحداهما انقرض جزء من الأخرى، وإن تفاوت الأجزاء الفرضية في كل مرتبة بحسب المقدار . قوله: (لمساواتها إلخ) والمساواة في الكمية هي الاتحاد فيها، فتكون تلك الأشياء متحدة فيها فهو استدلال بصدق المحدود على صدق الحد. قوله : (يخرج أكثر مباحث الكم المتصل أراد به المقدار بقرينة مقابلة الزمان، والمراد بمباحثهما مسائل الهندسة والحساب، فإن تينك المقدمتين مما يصدر به كتب المعلمين لكونهما من مبادئهما، وغيرها مما يذكر في الحكمة كالخواص الثلاثة من قبوله القسمة، ووجود المعادلة، وقبوله المساواة ،والمفاوتة، فإنها تتوقف على أن الكل أعظم من الجزء . قوله : ( وكثير من مباحث الزمان مثل إثبات كونه كما لقبوله المساواة والمفاوتة، وإثبات كونه مقداراً لأسرع الحركات لأنه يقدر به كل الحركات فيكون مقداراً لأسرعها لأن الأكبر يقدر بالأصغر دون العكس. قوله : ( والجسم) أي الطبيعي مثل قبول التخلخل والتكاثف والنمو والذبول، وامتناع التداخل فإنها مبنية على أن الكل أعظم من الجزء في المقدار. قوله : ( لكونه أي الكثير من مباحثهما راجعاً إلى البحث عن الكم المتصل مع قطع النظر عن خصوصية كونها زماناً، أو جسماً طبيعياً وفيه دفع لاستبعاد تعلق تينك المقدمتين بمباحثهما . المصادرة إذ المراد بوحدة الحقيقة الكمية هو المساواة في الكمية والكلام في بيان استلزام مساواة الأشياء لشيء في الكمية تساويها فليتأمل
His saying: 'Things equal in quantity, for example, to one thing' – it is said that the distance of fast motion and slow motion are not equal while they are equal in quantity to one thing, namely their time. The answer is to deny their equality to the time of the two motions in quantity, whether distance is considered a substance or an accident, because there is no room for numerical equality in terms of parts due to their absence, nor for quantitative equality due to lack of homogeneity between distance and time. If distance is made a substance, it is obvious; if it is made an accident, because it is a fixed quantity unlike time. Yes, they correspond to time in the sense that when a part of time ceases, a part of distance ceases. If this is made the meaning of equality, it does not prevent the two mentioned distances from being equal in the sense that when a part of one ceases, a part of the other ceases, even if the hypothetical parts differ in each order according to magnitude. His saying: 'For their equality etc.' – equality in quantity is unity in it, so those things are united in it, thus it is a deduction from the truth of the limited to the truth of the definition. His saying: 'The majority of the investigations of continuous quantity are derived' – he meant by it magnitude, by the context of contrasting with time. And what is meant by their investigations are the problems of geometry and arithmetic. For those two premises are what the books of the teachers begin with, because they are among their principles. And other things mentioned in wisdom like the three properties: its acceptance of division, existence of equation, and acceptance of equality and inequality – for they depend on the whole being greater than the part. His saying: 'And many investigations of time' – such as proving it is quantum by its acceptance of equality and inequality, and proving it is a measure of the fastest motions because every motion is measured by it, so it is a measure of the fastest because the greater is measured by the smaller, not vice versa. His saying: 'And the body' – meaning the natural body, such as accepting rarefaction and condensation, growth and decay, and impossibility of interpenetration – for they are based on the whole being greater than the part in magnitude. His saying: 'Because it' – i.e., many of their investigations – 'reverts to the investigation of continuous quantity, disregarding the particularity of being time or natural body' – and in that is a refutation of the improbability of the attachment of those two premises to their investigations. The begging of the question: for what is meant by the unity of quantitative essence is equality in quantity, and the discussion is about stating the implication of equality of things to one thing in quantity to their mutual equality. So reflect.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage provides commentary on the second self-evident proposition: things equal to the same thing are equal to each other. The commentator first addresses an objection: the distances of fast and slow motion are not equal to each other, yet they are both equal to their common time. The response is that this objection fails because distance and time are not homogeneous quantities; they cannot be compared in terms of numerical parts or quantitative magnitude. Rather, distance and time are related by correspondence (muṭābaqa): when a part of time passes, a corresponding part of distance passes, but this does not imply quantitative equality. If correspondence were taken as the meaning of equality, the two distances could still be considered equal in that same sense. The passage then explains that equality in quantity means unity in quantity, so the proposition is essentially a definitional tautology. It goes on to discuss the applications of these two premises (the whole is greater than the part and things equal to the same are equal) in geometry, arithmetic, time, and natural bodies. The commentator warns against begging the question by equating unity of quantitative essence with equality. The passage ends with an exhortation to reflect.

Key Terms
المساواة
الكمية
الانطباق
المطابقة
الكم المتصل
الهندسة
الحساب
الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things are equal to the same thing, they are always equal to each other, even if the 'same thing' is not homogeneous.
Correction: Equality requires homogeneity in quantity; distance and time are not homogeneous, so they only correspond, not equal. The commentator distinguishes between equality (which requires homogeneity) and correspondence (which does not).

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the objection that distances of fast and slow motion are not equal despite being equal to their common time?
A) He denies that they are equal to time.
B) He distinguishes between equality and correspondence, noting lack of homogeneity.
C) He asserts that they are indeed equal in all respects.
D) He argues that the objection confuses numerical and geometric equality.

Key Takeaway
The self-evident proposition that things equal to the same are equal only applies when the common thing is homogeneous in quantity; otherwise, it is a matter of correspondence, not equality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say two people are 'equal' in worth because of a shared attribute (e.g., wealth or status), do you actually check for homogeneity between that attribute and what you truly value? Where does this misapplication lead you?
?
Has there been a time you insisted that two things must be equal because they both relate to the same third thing, ignoring that the relationship was only correspondence? Can you name a real situation where this logical fallacy harmed your judgment?
?
The commentator warns against begging the question. In what area of your beliefs—about yourself, others, or Allah—do you assume the very thing you need to prove? Will you examine that assumption today?

The Third Self-Evident Proposition: One Body Cannot Be in Two Places
Commentary on the third self-evident proposition with contrapositive proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical reasoning behind the self-evident proposition that one body cannot occupy two places at the same time.

Core Idea
One body in two places leads to a logical contradiction.

This lesson explores a foundational principle of rational theology: a single body cannot be in two distinct places simultaneously. The argument is concise but powerful, using a reductio ad absurdum to show why any such claim leads to an absurd conclusion. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: a proposition, a conditional, and a consequence.
The passage includes a commentator's remark embedded in brackets.
2
Identify the proof strategy
The proof works by contradiction: assume the opposite, then show it leads to an absurdity. In this case, if a body were in two places, it would be indistinguishable from two separate bodies.
This is a common method in kalam.
3
Understand the contradiction
The absurdity is that the existence of the 'other body' would be both considered and not considered, which is a logical contradiction. This confirms the original proposition.
Contradiction proves impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الجسم الواحد لا يكون في آن واحد في مكانين (وإلا أي وإن لم يكن كما ذكرنا بل كان في مكانين (لم يتميز ذلك الجسم الواحد ( عن جسمين كذلك ) أي كائنين في آن واحد في مكانين (فالجسم الآخر معتبر وجوده ( وليس بمعتبر) إذ لم يتميز وجوده عن عدمه، قوله : (فالجسم الآخر معتبر وجوده ) أي الجسم الآخر من ذينك الجسمين الكائنين في مكانين.
His saying: 'One body cannot be in two places at one time' – otherwise, if it were not as we said but were in two places, then that one body would not be distinguished from two bodies, i.e., existing in two places at one time. So the other body's existence would be considered, but would not be considered, because its existence is not distinguished from its non-existence. His saying: 'So the other body's existence is considered' – meaning the other body from those two bodies existing in two places.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage explains the third self-evident proposition: one body cannot be in two places at the same time. The proof is by contrapositive: if it were in two places, it would be indistinguishable from two separate bodies each in a different place. Then the 'other body' (the second location's body) would have to be considered as existent, but also not considered, because its existence would not be distinguishable from non-existence. This shows the absurdity. The commentator adds a remark clarifying that 'the other body' refers to the second of the two bodies that would be in two places.

Key Terms
الجسم الواحد
آن واحد
مكانين
التمييز
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a non-physical body (e.g., soul) could be in two places at once, thereby invalidating the proposition.
Correction: The proposition specifically applies to physical bodies in physical places. The argument does not address non-physical entities; it is a rational principle about bodies.

Checkpoint
What is the logical consequence of a single body being in two places at the same time, according to the passage?
A) It would be identical to two bodies.
B) It would not be distinguished from two bodies, leading to a contradiction in existence.
C) It would expand to fill both places.
D) It would cease to exist.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of one body being in two places is proven by showing it leads to a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to split your existence—your time, attention, or loyalty—between two competing demands and felt the internal contradiction? How does this logical truth expose the limits of your creaturely capacity and call you to single-hearted devotion?
?
The proof shows that existence demands distinction: a thing cannot be both itself and another. Where in your life have you allowed contradictions in your beliefs about God or yourself to persist without resolution? Will you examine and reconcile them today?
?
If even a physical body cannot be in two places without contradiction, how much more should your soul avoid the impossible attempt to serve two masters? What is one area where you have been double-minded, and what concrete step will you take to root out that contradiction?

Defending the Self-Evidence of First Principles
The author refutes the objection that the three self-evident propositions require proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how self-evident propositions can have proofs implicitly present in the mind without becoming theoretical.

Core Idea
Self-evidence does not mean proof-free; proofs can be latent.

Having presented three self-evident propositions, the author now faces an objection: if they depend on proofs, they would be theoretical, not self-evident. In this passage, he defends their self-evidence by showing that the proofs are naturally noticed by rational people, even if not explicitly formulated. This lesson walks through the objection, the answer, and the clarification that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview of the argument. Notice the back-and-forth between objector and author.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the objector claims that if these propositions relied on proofs, they would be theoretical (naẓarī) rather than self-evident (badīhī).
The objection is implied in the text: 'كيف ولو توقفت عليها لكانت نظرية غير بديهية'
3
Understand the author's answer
The author responds by saying the proofs are 'noticed' (ملحوظة) by rational people, even if they cannot articulate them. The proofs are latent, not explicitly reasoned.
He gives examples like the saying about the whole and part.
4
Examine the preferred formulation
The author clarifies a better way to express the argument about one body in two places, showing that it reduces to contradiction.
This section addresses a potential misinterpretation.
5
Reflect on the final point
The passage ends by noting that these propositions are the most evident, called 'first of firsts' by sages, even if the proofs seem more obscure.
This reinforces that self-evidence does not require manifest proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيصدق عليه أنه موجود ومعدوم معاً ، وقيل : الأولى أن يقال : لو كان جسم واحد في آن واحد في مكانين لكان الواحد اثنين، فيكون وجود أحد المثلين وعدمه واحدا. ولما أمكن أن يقال : إن كل عاقل يعلم بالبديهة حقيقة هذه القضايا الثلاث وإن لم يخطر بباله تلك الحجج الدقيقة التي أوردتموها كيف ولو توقفت عليها لكانت نظرية غير بديهية؟ أشار إلى الجواب بقوله ( وهذه الاستدلالات) التي ذكرناها ( ملحوظة ) للعقلاء ) وإن عجز البعض عن تلخيصها في التعبير عنها ألا ترى إلى قولهم لو لم يكن الكل أعظم من الجزء لم يكن للجزء الآخر أثر البتة، ولو كان الشيء الواحد مساوياً لمختلفين لكان مخالفاً لنفسه فإنه إشارة إلى ما قررناه وإن لم تكن عبارته ملخصة كما لخصناها، لكن العبرة بالمعنى لا بالعبارة وليس يلزم من توقفها على هذه الحجج كونها نظرية لجواز كون الحجج ملحوظة بلا تجشم کسب جدید قوله : ( وقيل الأولى إلخ ) لما كان يرد على ظاهر ما في المتن أنا لا نسلم عدم تميز الواحد الكائن في مكانين عن الجسمين الكائنين في مكانين، لأن كل واحد الكائنين في مكانين لأن كل واحد من الجسمين متميز عن الآخر بشخصه بخلاف الجسم الواحد في مكانين ويندفع بأن المراد لا يتميز الواحد من حيث إنه واحد من الجسمين من حيث إنهما جسمان أي اثنان فيؤول إلى ما ذكره بقوله : والأولى إلا أن عبارته أصرح فتكون أولى . قوله : ( لكان الواحد اثنين لأن المفروض أنه واحد ، وقد حصل في مكانين، فيكون اثنين فالملازمة ضرورية والمناقشة بأنا لا نسلم ،ذلك لأنه موقوف على عديم جواز حصول الواحد في مكانين مكابرة . قوله : ( لجواز كون الحجج إلخ ) ولا يلزم منه كونها من القضايا الفطرية القياس، لأن تلك وتعمل فكر فتأمل، لكن بقي هاهنا شيء وهو أن هذه الاستدلالات أخفى من القضايا بلا شبهة، والحجة تجب أن تكون أبين من الدعوى، قالوا : فقد لاح أن أجلى البديهيات ما ذكرناه ولذلك سماه الحكماء بأول الأوائل
It is true that it is existent and non-existent together, and it was said: the better is to say: if one body were in one moment in two places, then one would be two, so the existence of one of the two likes and its non-existence would be one. And when it could be said: every rational person knows by intuition the reality of these three propositions even if those precise proofs you adduced do not occur to his mind; how [could they be self-evident] if they depended on them? He pointed to the answer by his saying: 'And these deductions that we mentioned are noticed by rational people' even if some are unable to summarize them in expression. Do you not see their saying: if the whole were not greater than the part, the other part would have no effect at all, and if one thing were equal to two different things, it would be contradictory to itself? For that is an indication of what we established, even if his expression is not summarized as we summarized it, but the consideration is of the meaning, not the expression. And it does not follow from their dependence on these proofs that they are theoretical, because it is possible that the proofs are noticed without seeking new acquisition. His saying: 'and it was said: the better etc.' because an objection arises on the apparent meaning of the text: we do not accept that the one in two places is not distinct from two bodies in two places, because each of the two bodies is distinct from the other by its person, unlike the one body in two places. And it is repelled by saying that what is meant is that the one as one is not distinct from the two bodies as two bodies, i.e., two, so it reduces to what he mentioned by his saying: 'the better', except that his expression is clearer, so it is better. His saying: 'then one would be two' because it is assumed that it is one, and it has occurred in two places, so it is two, so the implication is necessary, and the objection that we do not accept that because it depends on the impossibility of one thing being in two places is [an] unreasonable stubbornness. His saying: 'by virtue of the possibility of the proofs etc.' and it does not follow from it that they are among the innate dispositional propositions requiring syllogism, because those [proofs]... and reflect, but there remains here something: that these deductions are more obscure than the propositions without doubt, and the proof must be clearer than the claim. They said: it has become clear that the most evident of self-evident propositions is what we mentioned, and therefore the sages called it the first of firsts.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the self-evident (badīhī) nature of three fundamental propositions against an objection. The objection states: if these propositions depended on proofs, they would be theoretical (naẓarī), not self-evident. The author answers that the proofs are implicitly present in the minds of rational people, even if not explicitly articulated. He gives examples: common sayings like 'if the whole were not greater than the part...' show that the reasoning is latent. Then he clarifies the preferred formulation of the argument about one body in two places, rejecting a potential misinterpretation. Finally, he addresses the further objection that the proofs are more obscure than the propositions themselves, and concludes that these propositions are the most evident of all, called 'the first of firsts' by the sages.

Key Terms
قضايا بديهية (self-evident propositions)
استدلالات (deductions)
نظرية (theoretical)
فطرية (innate)
بداهة (self-evidence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-evident propositions require no reasoning at all.
Correction: The author shows that proofs can be implicitly present in the mind without making the propositions theoretical; self-evidence does not mean absence of any reasoning, but that the reasoning is innate and not consciously acquired.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection that the author refutes?
A) The three propositions are not known by rational people.
B) The three propositions require proof, so they are not self-evident.
C) The proofs for the three propositions are more evident than the propositions themselves.
D) The three propositions are not actually three but many.

Key Takeaway
Self-evident propositions can be known through implicit proofs that are naturally present in the mind, making them still self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your belief in the oneness of God (tawheed), do you rely on explicit proofs you can articulate, or are they so deeply known that you can't easily express them? Are you content with that implicit knowledge?
?
The sages called these propositions 'the first of firsts'. What is the first principle that governs your daily decisions? Have you ever examined if it is truly self-evident or just a habit?

The author says proofs are 'noticed' by rational people. Consider a time when you immediately knew something was true but could not explain why. Did you trust that intuition, or did you doubt it? What does this lesson teach you about the reliability of such intuitions?

The First Reason: Dependence on the Concept of Non-Existence
The author presents the first of four reasons why the law of excluded middle is non-certain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the law of excluded middle is considered non-certain due to its reliance on the problematic concept of non-existence.

Core Idea
The law of excluded middle is not absolutely certain because it depends on the elusive concept of non-existence.

The law of excluded middle appears self-evident, but classical scholars scrutinized its foundations. Here, the author presents the first of four objections: that this principle depends on understanding non-existence, which itself is conceptually problematic. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for logical certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the technical terms like تصديق, انفصال, منافاة, سلب, عدول, and حصر عقلي.
The passage is dense, so take your time to absorb the argument.
2
Identify the main claim
The author claims that the law of excluded middle is non-certain (غير يقيني). This lesson covers the first of four reasons. Locate the explicit statement of that reason in the text.
The reason is that this assent depends on the conception of non-existence (يتوقف على تصور المعدوم).
3
Understand the role of non-existence
The author argues that non-existence is part of the consequent (the 'it is not' part) in the disjunctive proposition. Consider why conceiving non-existence is problematic: it is not a positive concept and may be difficult to grasp.
The commentary emphasizes that the judgment is one of incompatibility between existence and non-existence.
4
Consider the privative vs. negative distinction
The text discusses whether 'it is not' should be taken as a privative (عدول) or a negative (سلب). This distinction affects whether the exclusivity is rational (حصر عقلي).
If taken as a negative, the proposition would involve four concepts (subject, two predicates, negation) rather than three, undermining rational exclusivity.
5
Reflect on the significance
Even if the proposition is problematic, the author notes that non-certainty is more general than lack of knowledge. This opens the door to further examination without rejecting the principle entirely.
The argument does not claim the law is false, only that it is not absolutely certain.

( وأما الثاني) أغنى كونه غير يقيني (فلوجوه ) أربعة ( الأول أنه ( أي هذا التصديق الذي هو قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون يتوقف على تصور (المعدوم الذي هو مفهوم قولنا لا يكون ضرورة توقف قوله : ( يتوقف على تصور (المعدوم هذه القضية منفصلة حقيقية، وإليه يشير الشارح بقوله فيما سيأتي لما أمكن الحكم بالانفصال بينهما، فالحكم هاهنا بالمنافاة. بین أن يكون، وأن لا يكون وخلاصة الحكم بالمنافاة بين هذا الشيء ،موجود وهذا الشيء معدوم فالمعدوم جزء من التالي، ولذا زاد الشارح قوله : وما يعتبر فيها وإن أخذ قضية حملية ردد بين محموليها نظراً إلى الظاهر قلنا : الشيء إما أن يكون وإما أن لا يكون، فحرف السلب جزء من المحمول الثاني سواء أخذ بطريق العدول أو بطريق السلب والحكم بالترديد بين المحمول المحصل، ونقيضه يقال : بعد تسليم ادعاء الأولوية فيها أن المراد عدم توقفه على قياس ملخص كما في تلك القضايا فتأمل . قوله : (بقي هاهنا شيء) قد يجاب بأن هذه الاستدلالات أبين من الدعوى لكن تلخيص العبارة فيها يحتاج إلى تأمل فليتأمل. قوله : ( وأما الثاني أعني كونه غير يقيني فلوجوه أربعة عدم اليقينية أعم من بقاء أصل التصديق، فلا ضير في دلالة الوجود على عدم حصول أصل العلم مع أن المدعى عدم اليقينية وهذا ظاهر لكن سياق كلام المستدل إلى هاهنا مشير إلى أنه معترف بالتحقيق، بل البداهة وهذا الكلام يدل على أنه لا يمكن التحقق فضلا عن البداهة والتدافع بينهما ظاهر. قوله : ( يتوقف على تصور المعدوم وإنه لا يتصور هذا ظاهر إذا أخذ لا يكون معدولة، وأما إذا أخذت سالبة كما هو الظاهر لأنها مناط صحة الحصر العقلي فلا لأن التصديق إنما يتوقف على التصورات الثلاث لا الأربع اللهم إلا أن تعتبر موجبة سالبة المحمول لأن الحصر حينئذ ليس إلا بملاحظة ،مساواتها للسالبة، فلا يكون الحصر عقلياً كما سيجيء نظيره في بحث الوجود
(And as for the second,) [it is] that it is non-certain, (for four reasons:) the first is that this assent which is our statement 'a thing is either existent or not' depends on the conception of non-existence, which is the meaning of our statement 'it is not', necessarily depending... His saying: 'depends on the conception of non-existence' – this proposition is a true disjunction, and the commentator points to it by his saying later: 'when it is possible to judge the separation between them, so the judgment here is incompatibility between being and not being.' The summary of the judgment of incompatibility between this thing existent and this thing non-existent: so non-existence is part of the consequent, and therefore the commentator added his saying: 'and what is considered in it, even if taken as a categorical proposition, he alternates between its two predicates in view of the apparent: we say: the thing is either existent or not existent, so the negative particle is part of the second predicate, whether taken by way of privation or negation, and the judgment is of alternation between the positive predicate and its negation.' It is said: after conceding the claim of priority in it, that what is meant is that it does not depend on a summarized syllogism as in those propositions, so reflect. His saying: 'there remains here something' – it may be answered that these deductions are clearer than the claim, but summarizing the expression in them requires reflection, so let one reflect. His saying: 'and as for the second, meaning it is non-certain, for four reasons' – non-certainty is more general than remaining of the original assent, so there is no harm in the indication of existence on not attaining original knowledge, while the claim is non-certainty, and this is apparent, but the context of the arguer's speech to here indicates that he acknowledges actual certainty (tahqīq), even intuitive knowledge, and this speech indicates that certainty is not possible, let alone intuitive, and the contradiction between them is apparent. His saying: 'depends on the conception of non-existence and that it is not conceivable' – this is apparent if 'it is not' is taken as a privative, but if taken as a negative, as is apparent because it is the basis of the validity of rational exclusivity, then no, because assent depends on three conceptions, not four, unless one considers [it as] a positive with a negative predicate, because exclusivity then is only by noting its equality to the negative, so it is not rational exclusivity, as will come its parallel in the discussion of existence.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents the first of four reasons why the second proposition (the law of excluded middle) is non-certain (ghayr yaqīnī). The reason is that this proposition depends on the concept of non-existence (al-maʿdūm), which itself is problematic to conceive. The commentary explains that the proposition is a true disjunction, and the judgment is one of incompatibility between existence and non-existence. Non-existence is part of the consequent. The discussion then addresses a potential confusion about whether the proposition is categorical or disjunctive, and clarifies that the negative particle is part of the second predicate. The commentary also responds to an objection about the clarity of the deduction, and notes that non-certainty does not mean total lack of knowledge. Finally, it considers whether 'it is not' should be taken as a privative or a negative, and concludes that if taken strictly as a negative, the proposition would not be a rational exclusivity (ḥaṣr ʿaqlī), because assent depends on three concepts, not four.

Key Terms
تصديق (assent)
انفصال (disjunction)
منافاة (incompatibility)
سلب (negation)
عدول (privation)
حصر عقلي (rational exclusivity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The law of excluded middle is absolutely certain and self-evident.
Correction: The author argues it is non-certain because it depends on the problematic concept of non-existence, which cannot be directly conceived.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the first reason that the law of excluded middle is non-certain?
A) It is a disjunctive proposition.
B) It depends on the concept of non-existence, which is hard to conceive.
C) It is not known by any rational person.
D) It requires four conceptions.

Key Takeaway
The first objection to the certainty of the law of excluded middle is its reliance on the problematic concept of non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that 'everything either exists or does not exist,' do you feel absolutely certain? This lesson asks you to examine the foundation of that certainty. How comfortable are you with the idea that even such a basic logical principle rests on a concept—non-existence—that you may not fully grasp? Will you pause to consider other assumptions you hold with confidence?
?
Think of a theological belief you consider self-evident. Can you identify a concept within it (like 'non-existence' or 'creation from nothing') that is actually elusive? Are you willing to question the certainty you attach to that belief, or do you prefer to avoid such scrutiny?
?
The author distinguishes between taking 'it is not' as a privative versus a negative. This shows that logical precision matters for certainty. In your own reasoning about Islamic creed, do you ever conflate different meanings of key terms? How can you be more careful in your own thinking?

The Objection and Reply on the Clarity of First Principles
Objection and reply on clarity of first principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the proofs for first principles are hidden, and the reply that the first principles are the clearest intuitions.

Core Idea
The clearest truths need no proof—only clarification.

In this lesson, we encounter a subtle objection: if a proof must be clearer than what it proves, how can we prove the most basic principles? The author addresses this by showing that the first principles are not proved but simply clarified. The passage distinguishes between the inherent clarity of intuitions and the need to articulate them clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: the inferences are more hidden than the propositions, but a proof must be clearer. Understand why this seems problematic.
The objection challenges the very possibility of proving first principles.
2
Examine the reply
The reply says the most evident intuitions (like the law of non-contradiction) are called 'first of the firsts' by philosophers. They are not proven but are the foundation.
Notice that the philosophers do not try to prove these principles; they merely point to their obviousness.
3
Reflect on the key insight
The commentator clarifies that the proofs are actually clearer than the claim, but their summarization requires reflection. The objection is based on a misunderstanding.
Ask yourself: Why does the objector think the proofs are hidden? Because they are not stated in full detail.

There remains here something: that these inferences are more hidden than the propositions without doubt, and the proof must be clearer than the claim. They said: Thus it has become clear that the most evident of intuitions is what we mentioned, and therefore the philosophers called it the first of the firsts. His saying: (there remains etc.) i.e., these inferences, summarized and unsummarized, are more hidden than those propositions without doubt, and the claim that these inferences are in themselves clearer—even though they are more hidden in being summarized—is obstinacy. His saying: (there remains here something) it may be answered that these inferences are clearer than the claim, but summarizing the expression in them requires reflection, so reflect.

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: the proofs (for necessary sciences) are more hidden than the proposition they prove, yet a proof must be more evident. The reply states that the first principles (the law of non-contradiction) are the clearest of intuitions, called 'first of the firsts' by philosophers. The commentator clarifies that the proofs are only summarizing what is inherently clear; the objection is obstinacy. He then notes that the inferences are indeed clearer than the claim, but their summarization requires careful reflection.

Correction: They are self-evident intuitions—the 'first of the firsts'—and are not proved but clarified.

Key Takeaway
The most basic principles of reason are self-evident and require no proof, only articulation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a time you rejected a simple truth because it seemed too obvious or easy. How might your intellectual pride have kept you from accepting the 'first of the firsts'? What step can you take today to embrace foundational truths without resistance?

The First Face: Impossibility of Conceiving the Nonexistent
Author presents first face of non-certainty with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conception of the nonexistent is deemed impossible and how the author defends this view against an objection.

Core Idea
Conception requires distinction, which implies existence; thus, conceiving the nonexistent leads to contradiction.

In this passage, the author presents the first of four arguments for the non-certainty of the first principle. He argues that the principle depends on conceiving the nonexistent, but such conceiving is impossible because it requires distinction and subsistence, turning the nonexistent into an existent. He then answers an objection that appeals to mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of argument.
The passage includes the main argument, an objection, and the author's response.
2
Identify the core argument
Notice how the author links conception with distinction and subsistence, leading to a contradiction.
3
Understand the objection
The opponent argues that conception is in the mind, so the nonexistent can be mentally present while externally absent.
This objection tries to avoid the contradiction.
4
Analyze the author's response
See how the author insists that the principle deals with absolute being, covering both mental and external existence, so the nonexistent cannot have any subsistence.
The response also addresses the distinction between privative and negative interpretations.
